data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transition%20metals" class="article__tags__link">Transition metals</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Sodium%20ion%20battery">Sodium ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrode+Reactions&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrode%20reactions">electrode reactions</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Mno+Nanoparticle&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_MnO%20nanoparticle">MnO nanoparticle</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nitrogen-doped+Carbon+Nanotube&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nitrogen-doped%20carbon%20nanotube">nitrogen-doped carbon nanotube</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great attentions as a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1-4)</a> However, further development of SIBs relies on great improvement of the electrode materials, as current electrode materials suffer from unstable rate capability, short cycle life, and large volume change during cycling in view of the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ion as compared to that of Li<sup>+</sup> ion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 5 to 8">(5-8)</a> Consequently, it remains challenging while becomes urgent to prepare high-activity electrode materials with desired excellent electrochemical performances. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, transition metal oxides have received growing attentions due to the increasing demand of green anodes for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10, 11)</a> MnO, because of its natural abundance, environmental benignity, low conversion potential, and voltage hysteresis, is promising as electrode materials of LIBs and supercapacitors, as well as attractive electrocatalysts for oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) and oxygen evolution reaction (OER). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 12 to 14">(12-14)</a> For example, reduced graphene oxide (RGO)–MnO–RGO sandwich nanostructures prepared through layer-by-layer electrodeposition method showed unprecedented rate capability and outstanding cyclic stability as anodes for LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Impregnation of MnO@N-doped carbon nanoparticles into a conductive graphene matrix presented high electrochemical performance for both LIBs and ORR. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> In addition, other MnO-based materials have also been prepared in recent years, such as three-dimensional arrays of MnO in carbon nanosheets, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> carbon-anchored MnO nanosheets, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> and MnO/carbon hybrid materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> However, little attention has been paid to the Na-storage behavior of MnO-related materials. What’s more, the electrochemical performances of MnO are currently impeded by the inherent poor electrical conductivity and rigorous volume change during the charge/discharge process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20, 21)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To date, one of the effective strategies to overcome the above bottlenecks is to construct nanocomposites through combining MnO nanoparticles within conductive scaffolds such as N-doped carbon materials, since the N-doped carbon materials possess the capability of accelerating electron transport, increasing the binding sites, and improving Na<sup>+</sup> ion adsorbability in the charging process due to the presence of N element. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22-24)</a> Along with the intensive studies, N-doped carbon materials derived from conjugated polymers, especially polyaniline (PANI), are regarded as new but significant members with promising applications in energy devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25-27)</a> The key point of choosing PANI as precursor is that through high-temperature treatment, the pivotal N element in its backbones can not only be introduced into the carbon framework at a relatively high content, but also stay stable under harsh working conditions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 28 to 31">(28-31)</a> Furthermore, using conjugated polymers as carbon precursors can lead to homogeneous distribution of nitrogen sites. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Meanwhile, one-dimensional N-doped carbon nanotubes (NDCT) with higher surface area and expanded interlayer distance can offer more active sites and short transport pathway for both electrons and Na<sup>+</sup> ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33, 34)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we designed and synthesized ultrasmall MnO nanoparticles (∼4 nm) homogeneously distributed on N-doped carbon nanotubes (NDCT@MnO) as anode materials for high-performance SIBs. NDCT@MnO nanocomposites were converted from pyrolysis of hydrothermally prepared Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanosheets on PANI nanotubes in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. Such NDCT@MnO nanocomposites, with a synergistic effect from each component, displayed many advantages including high surface area, increased diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, enhanced electrical conductivity and high stability of the MnO nanoparticles. As anode materials in SIBs, NDCT@MnO-7 (carbonized at 700 °C) displayed high rate capacity (709 and 292 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 0.1 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively) and superior long-term cycling life (capacity retention of 91% after 3000 cycles at a current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>).</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2-1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Reagents</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Aniline monomers were distilled under reduced pressure and blanketed with nitrogen before use. Iron(III) chloride hexahydrate (FeCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O), methyl orange (MO), concentrated sulfuric acid (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>), ammonium persulfate ((NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub>, APS), potassium permanganate (KMnO<sub>4</sub>) and ethanol were all AR grade (National medicine group chemical reagent Co., LTD) and used as received without further purification. Ultrapure water was used for all experiments.</div></div><div id="sec2-2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Preparation of Polyaniline (PANI) Nanotubes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">PANI nanotubes were synthesized using a modified self-degraded template method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> In a typical procedure, 0.2 mmol MO and 3 mmol FeCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O were added to 50 mL ultrapure water under magnetic stirring. To the above mixture, appropriate amount of APS was gradually added after 30 min and kept in ambient condition. After successive addition of 3 mmol aniline and 30 mL 0.5 M H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> aqueous solution, the reaction was allowed to be continued for 24 h under magnetic stirring. The resultant product was collected and repeatedly washed with ethanol and water mixture until the filtrate became colorless. At last, the precipitate was dried in a vacuum drier at 60 °C for overnight.</div></div><div id="sec2-3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Preparation of NDCT@MnO Nanocomposites</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical procedure, 0.2 g PANI nanotubes were dispersed in 70 mL 12 mM KMnO<sub>4</sub> aqueous solution at ambient condition under vigorous stirring to form a uniform solution. The solution was later transferred into a Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave. The autoclave was sealed quickly and maintained at 80 °C for 24 h in a digital temperature-controlled oven. The precipitate was filtered and washed adequately with ultrapure water and ethanol until the filtrate became colorless to obtain PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanocomposites. For the preparation of NDCT@MnO nanocomposites, the predried PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanocomposites were subjected to thermal treatments in a temperature-programmable tube furnace under high-purity N<sub>2</sub> (99.9999%) flow of 70 standard-state cubic centimeters per minute (sccm). The temperature was increased from room temperature to the presupposed temperature (600, 700, and 800 °C) at a ramp rate of 5 °C/min, and then kept at this temperature for 1.5 h. Denote the products at this process as NDCT@MnO-T (T = 6, 7, and 8 standing for 600, 700, and 800 °C). For comparison, free NDCT and MnO nanoparticles were fabricated by directly thermally treating PANI nanotubes at 700 °C for 1.5 h in N<sub>2</sub> (named as NDCT) and manganese acetate at 500 °C for 5 h in H<sub>2</sub> (5%)/N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere.</div></div><div id="sec2-4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Transmission electron microscopic (TEM) images were acquired with Topcon 002B transmission electron microscope. The Raman spectra were determined on a Renishaw in Via micro Raman spectroscopy system, using the TE air-cooled 576 × 400 CCD array in a confocal Raman system (wavelength: 532 nm). The incident laser power was kept at 0.1 mW, and total accumulation times of 5 s were employed. The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were conducted on a Rigaku/Max-3A X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ= 1.54178 Å), and the operation voltage and current were maintained at 40 kV and 40 mA, respectively. X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS) of the materials were obtained with PHI 5700 ESCA system equipped with an A1 Kα radiation as a source (hυ = 1486.6 eV). Nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms were measured on a QUADRASORB SI-KR/MP (Quantachrome) after heating the materials under vacuum at 120 °C. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA SDTQ600) was performed to evaluate the degradation of NDCT@MnO nanocomposite and determine the content of MnO. Samples weighing 2.5–5 mg were tested over a temperature range from room temperature to 800 °C at 10 °C/min under air atmosphere. For electrical conductivity measurement, a mixture of NDCT@MnO powder and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) with a weight ratio of 10:1 was compressed into a disc with a diameter of 1 cm and a thickness of 2 mm. Then the resistances were collected using a Keithley 2400 source meter with a four-point probe configuration at room temperature.</div></div><div id="sec2-5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrochemical Performances</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage behavior of the as-prepared materials was carried out by using 2032 type half-cells which were assembled in a glovebox filled with argon atmosphere. The NDCT@MnO electrode as anode was prepared by mixing NDCT@MnO (80 wt %), super P (10 wt %) as conductive additives, and carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC, 10 wt %) binder in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) solution to form a homogeneous slurry. Then the slurry was pasted onto a copper foil and dried for 12 h at 80 °C in a vacuum oven. Metallic sodium was selected as the reference/counter electrode, and glass fiber (Whatman) was used as the separator. The electrolyte was the mixture of ethyl carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (EC: DEC = 1:1, vol/vol) containing 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive. The mass loading of active material in electrode was ∼0.8 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The galvanostatic charge/discharge and cycling tests were performed on an automatic battery tester system (CT2001A, Wuhan LAND electronics, China) at the potential window between 0.01 and 3.0 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. Cyclic voltammetry was recorded on a CHI660E electrochemical workstation at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. Before it was tested for rate performance, the SIBs was electrochemically activated for 15 cycles under the 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup> in order to guarantee the sufficient penetration and transport of the electrolyte and the contact of Na<sup>+</sup> ions with the composites. This activation would make the charge/discharge process stable and reduce the capacity loss in the cycles even at high rate. Electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) were obtained by applying a sine wave with amplitude of 5 mV over the frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The preparation procedures of NDCT@MnO nanocomposites are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. Typically, PANI nanotubes were first synthesized by a modified self-degraded template method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Subsequently, Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanosheets were grown on PANI nanotubes by hydrothermal treatment of a solution containing PANI nanotubes and KMnO<sub>4</sub>. Then, the as-prepared PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanocomposites were thermally treated at various temperatures for 90 min in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere in order to complete the carbonization of PANI and reduction of Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> into MnO. During the calcination, PANI was carbonized and decomposed partially to create more defect sites along the surface of carbon nanotubes, and N element could be doped into the carbon framework, leading to N-doped carbon nanotubes (NDCT) with roughened surface. The defect sites and roughened surface play an important role in anchoring MnO nanoparticles onto the carbon nanotubes, which can further prevent the volume change of MnO nanoparticles during charge/discharge process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref17" aria-label="reference 1 and 17">(1, 17)</a></div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/medium/am-2017-09559r_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Illustration of the Synthetic Process of NDCT@MnO Nanocomposites As Anode Materials for SIBs, Which Involves a Hydrothermal Procedure of Preparing PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Nanocomposites and a Subsequent High-Temperature Carbonization Process for the Conversion into NDCT@MnO Nanocomposites</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b09559&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The crystal structures of the synthesized materials were examined by X-ray diffraction (XRD). As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, diffraction peaks observed from the PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanocomposites can be indexed to tetragonal Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> structure (JCPDS card No. 24–0734), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> where PANI can be barely distinguished due to its polymeric nature. After carbonization in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S1a</a>), distinct diffraction peaks at 34.9, 40.5, 58.7, 70.2, and 73.8° from NDCT@MnO nanocomposites can be well indexed to the (111), (200), (220), (311), and (222) crystal planes of cubic MnO (JCPDS card No. 07–0230). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> A broad peak centered at 24.7° is due to the (002) plane of amorphous carbon materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The above results indicate that PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> can be successfully transformed into NDCT@MnO by our pyrolysis technique, which is also confirmed by Raman study (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1b</a>). Minor peaks at 361 and 621 cm<sup>–1</sup> observed from PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> originate from crystalline Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> and the other Raman bands can be assigned to PANI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> For NDCT@MnO materials, two emerged broad peaks at 1358 and 1587 cm<sup>–1</sup> are due to the defect-induced (D) band and crystalline graphite (G) band of amorphous carbon, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The appearance of D band in NDCT@MnO nanocomposites indicates the presence of structural distortion caused by N-doping and edge defects in carbon materials, which is beneficial for the improvement of electrochemical performance and diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 38, 42 and 43">(38, 42, 43)</a> Moreover, intensity ratio of D band and G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is calculated to be 0.98 for NDCT@MnO-6, 0.91 for NDCT@MnO-7, and 0.70 for NDCT@MnO-8, which demonstrates that with increase in carbonization temperature, the graphitization degree of carbon nanotubes gets increased and the amount of defects is decreased. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref44" aria-label="reference 30 and 44">(30, 44)</a> The other small peak at 646 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be assigned to the Mn–O vibration in MnO. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Here, the MnO content in NDCT@MnO-6, NDCT@MnO-7 and NDCT@MnO-8 nanocomposites was determined to be 33.99, 42.06, and 25.58 wt %, respectively, from the thermogravimetric analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S2</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/medium/am-2017-09559r_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectra of the as-prepared PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites. (c) Mn 2p and (d) N 1s high-resolution XPS spectra of NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b09559&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further evaluate the surface area and porosity, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm measurement was conducted. The absorbed volumes exhibited by PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, NDCT, and NDCT@MnO materials are remarkably larger than that of pure MnO nanoparticles with a tiny specific surface area of 7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S1c,d and S3</a>). The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm of NDCT@MnO exhibits a typical type-IV behavior with a distinct hysteresis loop at relative pressure (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub>) ranging from 0.45 to 1,<sup>23</sup> implying that the NDCT@MnO nanocomposites contain a large number of mesopores (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S1c and S3a</a>). According to the pore size distribution profile (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S1d</a> and inset in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S3a</a>), the pore size is mainly distributed at ∼20 nm. From the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method, NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites possess a relatively high surface area of 234 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S4</a>), which however is lower than that of NDCT, possibly due to the filling of ultrasmall MnO nanoparticles into the mesopores of NDCT. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurement was also performed. Only Mn, O, C, and N elements are observed in the XPS survey spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S5a and S6a</a>) with an atomic ratio of Mn:O = 1:1, directly indicating the formation of MnO and successful incorporation of N into carbon framework. The high-resolution XPS spectrum of Mn 2p for NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c) exhibits two characteristic peaks at 641.8 eV (Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 653.8 eV (Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) with a spin-energy separation of 12.0 eV, which is another proof of the formation of MnO. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> The C 1s XPS spectrum of NDCT@MnO nanocomposites can be deconvoluted into four peaks at 284.7, 285.5, 286.5, and 289.1 eV (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S5b,c,d</a>), attributed to sp<sup>2</sup>C-sp<sup>2</sup>C, N-sp<sup>2</sup>C, N-sp<sup>3</sup>C, and C = O bonds, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46, 47)</a> Similarly, the surface nitrogen functional groups (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S6b,c</a>) can be deconvoluted into four bands centered at 398.4, 400.6, 402.0, and 403.2 eV, corresponding to the Pyridinic-N, Pyrrolic-N, Graphitic-N, and Oxidized-N, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Remarkably, with increase in the carbonization temperature, Graphitic-N and Oxidized-N become dominant, accompanied by the reduced content of Pyridinic-N and Pyrrolic-N due to their low thermal stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S6d and Table S1</a>). The presence of N element in carbon nanotubes can enhance the electronic conductivity of the as-prepared nanocomposites. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Furthermore, Pyridinic-N and Pyrrolic-N can not only create some defects in carbon nanotubes, but also provide more transport channels and active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Meanwhile, high content of Graphitic-N can also enhance the reactivity, electrical conductivity and the transfer of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, which is beneficial to improving the rate capability of the anode materials for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> These can be proved by electrical conductivity and electrochemical impedance spectrum (EIS) studies. Electrical conductivity measurement was conducted by a four-point probe method (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Table S2</a>). It is found that PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> has very limited conductivity (&lt;10<sup>–6</sup> S m<sup>–1</sup>), while NDCT@MnO nanocomposites show greatly improved conductivity (0.20–0.37 S m<sup>–1</sup>). Electrochemical impedance spectra were collected based on the assembled cells (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S7 and Table S3</a>). According the reported method, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) were calculated. It can be seen that <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of NDCT@MnO nanocomposites are substantially smaller than those of PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, NDCT, and MnO, while <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> values of NDCT@MnO nanocomposites are obviously larger. As compared to the individual NDCT and MnO, a synergistic effect between MnO and NDCT can enhance the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> by at least 3 orders of magnitude. Here, NDCT@MnO-7 shows the smallest charge transfer resistance and largest diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Above results reveal that the electrical conductivity, charge transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion can be highly enhanced in the NDCT@MnO nanocomposites.</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphology characterization of as-prepared materials was performed by transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The as-prepared PANI samples are nanotubes with roughened surface and an inner diameter of ∼70 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S8</a>). Through the hydrothermal technique, nanosheet-like Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> from the decomposition of KMnO<sub>4</sub> is homogeneously anchored to the PANI nanotubes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). A closer look from the high resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (inset in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a) reveals an interplanar spacing of 0.26 nm, corresponding to the (211) planes of Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> crystals. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> Interestingly, ultrasmall MnO nanoparticles are evolved from Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanosheets by the calcination treatment, and the inner diameter of NDCT is shrunk to ∼50 nm (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S9</a>). Of note is that the MnO nanoparticles are firmly anchored on the NDCT. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c shows clear lattice fringes of single MnO nanoparticle for NDCT@MnO-7 with interplanar distances of 0.22 nm, attributed to the (200) plane of cubic MnO. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> The average size of the obtained ultrasmall MnO nanoparticles for NDCT@MnO-7 is ∼4 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S8b</a>). However, the size of MnO nanoparticles is obviously larger after calcination at 800 °C than that of NDCT@MnO-6 and NDCT@MnO-7 due to the Ostwald ripening effect (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S9</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> Scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) image of a single NDCT@MnO-7 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S8c</a>) with corresponding elemental mapping in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d indicates that C, N, O and Mn element are homogeneously distributed in the NDCT@MnO nanocomposites.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/medium/am-2017-09559r_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. TEM and HRTEM images of (a) PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and (b, c) NDCT@MnO-7. (d) Energy-dispersive elemental mapping of C, N, O, and Mn of a single NDCT@MnO-7.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b09559&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical data of the as-prepared samples as anode materials for SIBs were displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figures S10, S11, and S12</a>. Here, the gravimetric capacity was calculated from the mass of active materials (the total mass of the as-prepared electrode materials without the inclusion of binders and carbon additives). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of NDCT@MnO-7 in the first three cycles in the potential range of 0.01–3.0 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Three reduction peaks at 0.92, 0.67, and 0.35 V emerged in the first cathodic scan result from the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into NDCT@MnO structure, typically irreversible decomposition of electrolyte and the formation of solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) layer at the surface of the electrode, and the initial reduction of Mn<sup>2+</sup> ions to Mn<sup>0</sup> (MnO + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>–</sup> → Mn + Na<sub>2</sub>O). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49 ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 49, 54 and 55">(49, 54, 55)</a> These reduction peaks are merged into one reduction peak at 0.712 V in the subsequent CV cycles, which should be attributed to the enhanced kinetics and alterations in the microstructure of the electrode caused by the formation of metallic manganese and Na<sub>2</sub>O after the first sodiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref21 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref21 ref45" aria-label="reference 16, 21 and 45">(16, 21, 45)</a> For the anodic process, an oxidation peak around 1.21 V can be ascribed to the oxidation of Mn<sup>0</sup> to Mn<sup>2+</sup> (Mn + Na<sub>2</sub>O → MnO + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>–</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> Besides, the CV curves almost overlap with each other during the subsequent CV cycles, implying the good reversibility of NDCT@MnO-7 during the insertion/extraction process of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/medium/am-2017-09559r_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage performance of NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites. (a) Cyclic voltammetry curves scanned at a rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. (b) Discharge/charge profiles of NDCT@MnO-7 for the first three cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling performance of NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability in the potential window of 0.01–3.0 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. (e) Long-term cycling stability of NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites at a current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b09559&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b displays the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of NDCT@MnO-7 for the first three cycles in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The first discharge and charge cycle delivers irreversible specific capacities of 1540 and 615 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with an initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 47%, which may be attributed to the irreversible reaction of the decomposition of electrolyte and the formation of SEI film. For the second cycle, the discharge and charge capacities are reduced to 775 and 605 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, leading to an increased CE of 78%. It is particularly encouraging that the CE can approach ∼100% after several cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), an indication of high reversible capacities during the sodiation/desodiation process. According to previous reports, the electrolyte would be difficult to penetrate into the electrode under low current density, which is expected to increase the polarization of the electrode that leads to low CE. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Therefore, an activation process is suggested in order to improve the rate capability and cyclic stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58 ref59 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 58 to 60">(58-60)</a> Herein, the rate performances were recorded after a 15-cycle activation of the assembled SIBs. For NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d), the average capacities can reach 709, 541, 463, 360, and 292 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 2, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> with ten cycles at each stage, respectively. More importantly, the reversible capacity can quickly recover to 686 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density is switched back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, illustrating a high rate activity of NDCT@MnO during the sodiation/desodiation process. It is noted that the reversible capacity of PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanocomposites at 0.1–5 A g<sup>–1</sup> decreases from 178 to 26 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S12</a>), which is far away from that of NDCT@MnO nanocomposites, suggesting the inactive nature of PANI@Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as anode materials for SIBs. This low reversible capacity is not enough for practical application in SIBs. In addition, NDCT@MnO nanocomposites exhibited markedly superior Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior to individual NDCT and MnO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). In comparison to NDCT@MnO-7, the decreased reversible capacities at various current densities for NDCT@MnO-6 and NDCT@MnO-8 might be attributed to the small BET surface area, low amount of MnO, low electrical conductivity and decreased charge transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S7</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Tables S2 and S3</a>). The cyclic performance of the NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c displays that CE reaches almost 100% after 20 cycles, and the reversible capacity retains stable at 536 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> without obvious fading after 250 cycles. To further study the long-term cycling performance of the as-fabricated NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites, the durability at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> was recorded (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). The reversible capacity can reach 301 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and be kept at 273 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (91% retention) after 3000 cycles with nearly ∼100% CE, indicating good cycling stability. Through a careful comparison, the electrochemical Na-storage properties of the NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites can outperform most of transition metal oxide anodes for SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Table S5</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref23 ref54 ref55 ref61 ref62 ref63 ref64 ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68 ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref23 ref54 ref55 ref61 ref62 ref63 ref64 ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68 ref69" aria-label="reference 11, 23, 54, 55 and 61 to 69">(11, 23, 54, 55, 61-69)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In order to better understand the electrochemical processes, with NDCT@MnO-7 as a typical sample, the cells were disassembled and characterized by ex situ XRD and HRTEM techniques at selected sodiation (discharge to 0.01 V) and desodiation (charge to 3.0 V) states after the first full process. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, diffraction peaks of MnO are replaced by metallic Mn when the electrode is fully discharged to 0.01 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>O phase cannot be observed in the XRD pattern possibly due to its poor crystallization or limited content. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a> After fully charged to 3.0 V, peaks of MnO reappear, demonstrating the reversal of the discharge process for a return to the initial form of the material. TEM images of the NDCT@MnO-7 display no obvious change in the size and morphology at selected discharge and charge states, confirming the robustness of the MnO nanoparticles supported on NDCT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S14</a>). HRTEM images of NDCT@MnO-7 electrodes at the same discharge and charge states were also collected (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). After the discharge process, an observed interplanar spacing of 0.21 nm can be indexed to the (221) planes of Mn (JCPDS card No. 33–0887). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Notably, the particle size of Mn is similar to that of MnO, indicating that MnO nanoparticles anchored on the carbon framework can effectively mitigate volume change during sodiation. When charged to 3.0 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), the interplanar spacing of 0.22 nm is consistent with the (200) plane of MnO, an indication of successful reversible conversion from Mn to MnO. These results confirm the proposed electrochemical reactions and significant role of MnO during charge and discharge process using NDCT@MnO-7 as the anode materials for SIBs.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/medium/am-2017-09559r_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XRD patterns of the NDCT@MnO-7 electrodes in the states of sodiated (after the 1st discharge) and desodiated (after the 1st charge), HRTEM images of (b) sodiated (after the 1st discharge) electrode and (c) desodiated (after the 1st charge) electrode of NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/asset/images/large/am-2017-09559r_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b09559&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The long-term cycling stability of NDCT@MnO-7 can also be reflected from the structure characterization after charge–discharge tests (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S15</a>). NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites are almost unchanged after 3000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, where MnO nanoparticles are still homogeneously distributed on NDCT without obvious aggregation. Well-resolved periodic lattice fringes with interplanar spacing of 0.22 nm corresponding to the (200) plane of cubic MnO phase can be clearly observed, an indication that the crystallinity of MnO is also not influenced. We think the defect sites and roughened surface of the NDCT can account for the size and morphology stability of these MnO nanoparticles during the charge–discharge process. Also, from the HR-TEM images shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b and c, the very slight difference in the dominant lattice fringes between MnO and Mn may be another reason for the almost unchanged size and morphology of the MnO nanoparticles. Based on above results, the improved electrochemical performance of NDCT@MnO nanocomposites can be understood from a synergestic effect between MnO nanoparticles and NDCT: NDCT provides nucleation sites for MnO during the synthesis, maintains the stability of the MnO nanoparticles during the charge–discharge process, affords the improved surface area and electrical conductivity, and ultrafine MnO nanoparticles supported on the NDCT can greatly enhance diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and thus the electrochemical performances (rate capability and cycling life) as anode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">4 Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we demonstrate the promising Na-storage behavior of ultrasmall and stable MnO nanoparticles uniformly supported on N-doped carbon nanotubes (NDCT@MnO). The electrochemical reactions using NDCT@MnO-7 (carbonized at 700 °C in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere) as anode materials for SIBs have been revealed by ex situ XRD and HRTEM studies at selected sodiation and desodiation states. The as-prepared NDCT@MnO nanocomposites display exceptional rate capability (709 and 292 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 0.1 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively) and ultralong cycling life (capacity retention of 91% after 3000 cycles at a current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>). The enhanced rate performances and durable long-term cycling stability of NDCT@MnO-7 nanocomposites can be attributed to the improved surface area, higher diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, and enhanced electrical conductivity due to the distinctive structure and compositional characteristics. This work recognizes MnO as a promising anode material for SIBs, and reveals that distinct structure features of ultrasmall MnO nanoparticles supported on highly conductive N-doped carbon nanotubes are significant for high-performance SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b09559" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b09559</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Figure S1–S15, and Table S1–S5 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b09559/suppl_file/am7b09559_si_001.pdf">am7b09559_si_001.pdf (5.39 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b09559" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">This study examd. the electrochem. performance of hollow carbon nanospheres as anode material for sodium ion batteries.  The nanospheres were prepd. by a facile, sustainable hydrothermal procedure.  Findings revealed the excellent cycling stability and rate capability of the nanospheres when used as anode materials for the sodium-based batteries.  Mass transport and storage were effectively enhanced through addnl. refinement of material parameters, including morphol. and size, resulting in a considerable improvement of the electrochem. performance both for lithium and sodium.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpFHFn_p5w7jrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg4CwXt1TczHQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLbF&amp;md5=24c5da0ec94442593a692757cb3a0c66</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501867"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ren, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Peng, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Integration: An Effective Strategy to Develop Multifunctional Energy Storage Devices</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljEz4is2R-UMg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201601621"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Foster, C.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphene-Rich Wrapped Petal-Like Rutile TiO2 tuned by Carbon Dots for High-Performance Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yan; Foster, Christopher W.; Banks, Craig E.; Shao, Lidong; Hou, Hongshuai; Zou, Guoqiang; Chen, Jun; Huang, Zhaodong; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here, CDs (C dots), for the 1st time, are proposed as designer additives for graphene-rich petal-like rutile TiO2 (termed: G/P-RTiO2).  As effective functionalization inducers, they can create a petal-like structure consisting of nanoneedles, which are advantageous for shortening the diffusion pathways of the Na ions.  Through thermal annealing, a favorable and ultrathin graphitic C (6-7 layers) is produced to significantly enhance the cond. of the ensemble, leading to fast electron mobility.  When applied as an anode in SIBs (Na-ion batteries), the G/P-RTiO2 delivers a high capacity of 245.3 mAh/g and good initial Coulombic efficiency of ∼50.6% at a c.d. of 0.25 C (83.75 mA/g).  The reversible capacity of 144.4 mAh/g is obsd. at 2.5 C (837.5 mA/g) after 1100 cycles with full capacity retention; even, a capacity of 74.6 mAh/g is maintained after 4000 cycles at extremely high rate 10 C (3350 mA/g) with a retention of 94.4%, outstandingly, showing a remarkable long-term cycle stability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqX32tIHCU-4rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljEz4is2R-UMg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsVChtLvM&amp;md5=1bb3be4ec4f3c103c91fe55f624a1674</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA07452A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xia, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">One-Dimensional Hybrid Nanocomposite of High-Density Monodispersed Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Nanoparticles and Carbon Nanotubes for High-Capacity Storage of Lithium and Sodium</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">One-dimensional hybrid nanocomposite of high-density monodispersed Fe3O4 nanoparticles and carbon nanotubes for high-capacity storage of lithium and sodium</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Xiujuan; Liu, Xiaojie; Wang, Gang; Xia, Yuan; Wang, Hui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">47</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18532-18542</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we report a simple but effective method to fill carbon nanotube (CNT) channels with a dense distribution of Fe3O4 nanoparticles, to produce Fe3O4@CNT.  The resulting novel heterogeneous nanostructure displayed multiple attractive features as an anode material in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  For example, this nanostructure displayed voids that apparently effectively prevented active material from aggregating and helped to accommodate their volumetric expansion during the cycling process.  In addn., most solid electrolyte interphases (SEIs) formed on the surfaces of the CNT shells instead of on individual Fe3O4 nanoparticles, limiting the amt. of SEIs and increasing the stability of the Fe3O4 nanoparticles.  As a result, when tested for LIBs, the Fe3O4@CNT nanocomposite exhibited an excellent rate capacity and cycling performance in half and full cells.  A high reversible capacity of 720 mA h g-1 was obtained after 200 cycles at 1 A g-1, and when the c.d. was increased to 8 A g-1, the specific capacity was 400 mA h g-1.  As for SIBs, a specific capacity of 377 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 was achieved after 300 cycles.  The facile synthetic route and unique nanostructure design may be extended to high-performance anode materials for LIBs and SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpPJRClqmvTG7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljEz4is2R-UMg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhslGgsbrK&amp;md5=e63c2974a1c64aa5ee40bb0393e20173</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1039/C5CC01152C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kuo, C.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Mechanistic Investigation of Water Oxidation Catalyzed by Uniform, Assembled MnO Nanoparticles</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Kyoungsuk; Seo, Hongmin; Hayashi, Toru; Balamurugan, Mani; Jeong, Donghyuk; Go, Yoo Kyung; Hong, Jung Sug; Cho, Kang Hee; Kakizaki, Hirotaka; Bonnet-Mercier, Nadege; Kim, Min Gyu; Kim, Sun Hee; Nakamura, Ryuhei; Nam, Ki Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of active water oxidn. catalysts is crit. to achieve high efficiency in overall water splitting.  Recently, sub-10-nm-sized monodispersed partially-oxidized manganese oxide nanoparticles were shown to exhibit not only superior catalytic performance for oxygen evolution, but also unique electrokinetics, compared to their bulk counterparts.  In the present work, the water-oxidizing mechanism of partially-oxidized MnO nanoparticles was investigated using integrated in-situ spectroscopic and electrokinetic analyses.  It is successfully demonstrated that, in contrast to previously reported manganese (Mn)-based catalysts, Mn(III) species are stably generated on the surface of MnO nanoparticles via a proton-coupled electron transfer pathway.  Furthermore, it is confirmed as to MnO nanoparticles that the one-electron oxidn. step from Mn(II) to Mn(III) is no longer the rate detg. step for water oxidn. and that Mn(IV)=O species are generated as reaction intermediates during catalysis.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJVa_16A1817Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljXkrpsAim4JA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitFGqsr3M&amp;md5=ab5dda6dfc053547eac804f331eba5f9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA08330G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, M.-Q.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Flexible High-Energy Asymmetric Supercapacitors Based on MnO@C Composite Nanosheet Electrodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing asym. supercapacitors (ASCs) is a promising alternative to achieve both wide voltage windows and high energy densities to meet the application requirements of hybrid vehicles and renewable energy systems.  However, a major limitation of high performance asym. supercapacitors lies in neg. electrode materials which possess relatively low specific capacitance compared to pos. electrode materials.  Herein, a novel MnO@C composite nanosheet array directly grown on conductive carbon cloth as a neg. electrode is prepd. successfully, which exhibits a large specific capacitance of 662.9 F g-1 at a c.d. of 3.7 A g-1 and an areal capacitance of 716 mF cm-2 at a c.d. of 4 mA cm-2. In addn., the ASC exhibits superior flexibility and mech. stability even under severe bending states.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium ion batteries have attained great success in commercialization owing to their high energy d.  However, the relatively delaying discharge/charge severely hinders their high power applications due to intrinsically diffusion-controlled lithium storage of the electrode.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To tackle the issues of inferior cycling stability and low cond. for MnO as an anode material for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and as a catalyst for oxygen redn. reaction (ORR), a facile and effective strategy is explored to confine N-doped carbon-coated MnO nanoparticles in a conductive graphene matrix.         </div><div class="casAbstract">It is a challenge to meld the energy of secondary batteries with the power of supercapacitors. For example, 1332 mA-h/g is reached at 0.1 A/g, 567 mA-h/g at 5 A/g, and 285 mA-h/g at 20 A/g with negligible degrdn. at 500 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing electrode materials with high rate as well as prolonged cycle is particularly necessary for the ever-growing market penetration of elec. vehicles and hybrid elec. vehicle.  Herein, a facile and efficient strategy is demonstrated to synthesize MnO/C hybrid via freeze-drying followed by thermal treatment in N2 atmosphere.  The MnO nanosheets are firmly anchored onto carbon layers to form MnO/C hybrid.  When used as an anode in lithium-ion batteries, the typical MnO/C hybrid displays a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 83.1% and delivers a high capacity of 1449.8 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.3 A g-1.  Furthermore, the typical MnO/C hybrid can still maintain significantly high capacity of 1467.0 mAh g-1 after 2000 cycles at 5 A g-1, which may be the best performance reported so far for MnO-based materials.  The superior electrochem. performance of the MnO/C hybrid may be attributed to its unique microstructure features such as effective conductive pathway of carbon sheets, firm connection between MnO and carbon sheets, and small-sized MnO.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo-w9Zxyp4m-LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljbv7iPikbAdQ"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Crosslinking-derived MnO/carbon hybrid with ultrasmall nanoparticles for increasing lithium storage capacity during cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kang, Danmiao; Liu, Qinglei; Si, Rui; Gu, Jiajun; Zhang, Wang; Zhang, Di</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">MnO/C hybrid with ultra-small MnO nanoparticles (5.2 nm) embedded in porous carbon is fabricated and applied as the anode material for lithium ion batteries.  The MnO/C hybrid electrode exhibits excellent specific capacity of 820 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 and also outstanding rate performance with specific capacity of 483 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 5000 mA g-1.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reconstruction of Conformal Nanoscale MnO on Graphene as a High-Capacity and Long-Life Anode Material for Lithium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Yongming; Hu, Xianluo; Luo, Wei; Xia, Fangfang; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2436-2444</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">To tackle the issue of inferior cycle stability and rate capability for MnO anode materials in lithium ion batteries, a facile strategy is explored to prep. a hybrid material consisting of MnO nanocrystals grown on conductive graphene nanosheets.  The prepd. MnO/graphene hybrid anode exhibits a reversible capacity as high as 2014.1 mAh g-1 after 150 discharge/charge cycles at 200 mA g-1, excellent rate capability (625.8 mAh g-1 at 3000 mA g-1), and superior cyclability (843.3 mAh g-1 even after 400 discharge/charge cycles at 2000 mA g-1 with only 0.01% capacity loss per cycle).  The results suggest that the reconstruction of the MnO/graphene electrodes is intrinsic due to conversion reactions.  A long-term stable nanoarchitecture of graphene-supported ultrafine manganese oxide nanoparticles is formed upon cycling, which yields a long-life anode material for lithium ion batteries.  The lithiation and delithiation behavior suggests that the further oxidn. of Mn(II) to Mn(IV) and the interfacial lithium storage upon cycling contribute to the enhanced specific capacity.  The excellent rate capability benefits from the presence of conductive graphene and a short transportation length for both lithium ions and electrons.  Moreover, the as-formed hybrid nanostructure of MnO on graphene may help achieve faster kinetics of conversion reactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqSjyTOe_ODXLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhp6IoUs4yItA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhvVWnsL%252FE&amp;md5=993266e37afcdc7949e8735381125094</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA06095H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhancing the Performance of MnO by Double Carbon Modification for Advanced Lithium-Ion Battery Anodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhancing the performance of MnO by double carbon modification for advanced lithium-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang, Xiaojian; Yu, Wei; Wang, Hui; Xu, Huayun; Liu, Xizheng; Ding, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
